Time Published: 2026-04-30T19:43:01Z
Full Content:
When May 2026 arrives, changes across Ethereum go deep into its bones. Instead of just small investors pushing talk, three forces now steer things: how founder Vitalik Buterin shifts his holdings, big players such as Tom Lee’s Bitmine buying up supply fast, along with unseen upgrades to the code itself. Since “Glamsterdam” went live – and “Hegotá” is waiting in the wings – pressure builds quietly beneath the surface. What comes next might shake ETH’s lowest value point hard enough to set terms for years after. What grabs blockchain detectives every time? Vitalik Buterin keeps moving. Fresh numbers from Arkham show something odd: his wallet, tagged “Vitalik.eth,” now floods with tokens tied to Internet noise. Project builders toss huge chunks of meme-driven coins straight into that account – no questions asked. Their logic? Seeing his name listed might just spark trust, like an invisible nod nobody gave but everyone sees. Movement there isn’t quiet – it echoes. Still, Buterin keeps doing the same thing every time. Instead of holding on, he moves step by step to cash out. Data from Lookonchain caught him turning vast amounts of random tokens – like ASTEROID OG and similar ones – into ETH. One day alone showed about $350,000 in untouched coins traded straight for Ethereum. vitalik.eth (@VitalikButerin) has been continuously selling the meme tokens he received for free, and it looks like he's clearing out his wallet.In the past 24 hours, he has gained 114,566 $USDC and 155 $ETH ($355K) by selling those free meme tokens.https://t.co/EWizSRolCi pic.twitter.com/9M2b80BUI8 Sales like these do more than one thing. One reason behind them is supporting causes Buterin cares about – think of projects tied to biotechnology or open science efforts built on blockchains. Instead of holding tokens, he trades meme-based ones for Ether, feeding what some might dismiss as playful money into something with a deeper function. In doing so, energy that could vanish into hype gets pulled toward stability. Each transaction quietly supports demand for ETH, even if only by a little. It also shows Ethereum stands for creation, not just quick wins or luck-driven plays. Read More: Top 5 Wild Ethereum Price Predictions for 2026: From Realistic to Moonshot Scenarios Even as Buterin takes center stage socially, real movement behind prices comes from quiet big players – those nameless giants and firms working unseen. A recent Lookonchain highlight stands out: around $850 million worth of ETH vanished from central exchanges in late April 2026, swept away by only a few major hands. Price may sit still or dip a little, yet strong buying underneath tells another tale – one where serious holders steadily pull supply offline. That tension between flat charts and hidden accumulation? It’s a textbook bullish split forming quietly beneath the surface. Out of nowhere, old crypto wallets from the 2014–2015 fundraising days of Ethereum have woken up. Arkham Intelligence spotted movement – dormant since back then, now active again. A single wallet pushed 10,000 ETH into staking after sitting still for 11 years. These long-gone holders are returning. Their timing hints at trust in what comes next. Ethereum’s planned improvements in 2026 seem like a turning point. For them, it might be the moment when earning rewards finally feels secure. It’s Tom Lee who stands out among big names in 2026, running things at Bitmine. Though many recognize him from CNBC spots and talk about market supercycles, what his company does on blockchain reveals deeper clues. His moves online paint a clearer picture than headlines ever could. Related: Ethereum Updates 2026: Upcoming Changes That Could Transform the Network Now shaping the backbone of digital systems, Bitmine started as just a trading hub. Holding close to 4.17 million ETH – that’s about 3.45 percent of every unit in existence – it sits on one of the densest personal stockpiles around. With more than 1.25 million ETH locked into staking, its role shifts beyond storage. Functioning much like a reserve authority within open networks, it fuels access to funds while pulling in close to $100 million each year through staked returns. WHALES ARE DOUBLING DOWN ON ETHThese whale addresses just purchased a further $44.8M of ETH this morning.Judging by the purchase pattern, this appears to be Bitmine. Is Tom Lee doubling down? pic.twitter.com/VXWbvC2Oj9 Come 2026, Lee’s argument rests on one core idea: too little Ethereum floating around when so much gets locked away. Big players such as Bitmine and BlackRock stash ETH in staking pools or exchange-traded funds (ETFs) – taking it off the open market. Meanwhile, busy times on the network destroy more of it via EIP-1559, slowly shrinking what remains tradeable. With fewer coins showing up on exchanges, pressure builds beneath the price. That tightening pool, not speculation, may drive Ethereum into the $5,000–$7,000 zone by year-end 2026. Scarcity whispers louder than hype. One step at a time, Ethereum shifts beyond what once felt like growing pains. In 2026, plans reach further than any before. Maturity takes shape through speed, stability, and strength. What began as an experiment now stands more solid – less trial, more function. Early in April 2026 came Glamsterdam, bringing locked-in Proposer-Builder Separation – known as ePBS. Up until then, building blocks leaned toward central control, easily twisted by strong MEV-focused bots. Thanks to ePBS, that task now spreads across many players, blocking one actor from jumping ahead of trades. Confidence among big financial users in Ethereum’s fairness got stronger because of it. Later in 2026, the path toward true independence turns on the Hegotá update. Instead of today’s Merkle Patricia Trees, it brings in Verkle Trees – shrinking what nodes must check. The shift sounds subtle, yet cuts proof sizes sharply. Though buried deep in code, its impact ripples outward fast. Running an Ethereum node might soon fit inside a regular phone. That happens because stateless clients reduce the amount of data that needs to be saved. A lightweight setup could work without requiring large amounts of memory. Some devices never handled nodes before – they can now join. Less storage demand opens new possibilities. Old limits start fading when tech adapts like this. One step beyond, Hegotá teams up with the ZKVM effort to stretch system capacity past ten million actions each second across both base layer and expansion layers. What unfolds next depends on how tightly these parts link together under pressure. Close to May 2026, forces start lining up – quiet at first, then harder to ignore. One thing follows another, building pressure beneath the surface. Prices begin to twitch, not by accident. Each piece adds weight, even if unseen. What’s been slow to show now pushes into view. Word has it that after seeing what Bitmine did, a few medium-sized pension plans might soon shift 1 to 2 percent of their holdings into ETH ETFs. Not everyone is moving at once, yet the pattern suggests quiet momentum behind the scenes. Every time he trades memecoins into ETH, Vitalik nudges perception – quietly shaping how people see the token. Not as noise, but something that builds. His moves don’t shout; they settle into view like weathered facts. Slowly, the idea sticks: value flows here because it works. What was playful becomes weighty without announcement. Action after action piles up, meaning. The pattern isn’t loud – it’s just there, like footprints leading somewhere obvious only later. Surprisingly low exchange liquidity shows up in Lookonchain figures – Ethereum’s presence on trading platforms hasn’t been this small since 2016. While most assets sit elsewhere, the trend quietly highlights a shift over the years. Notably, the tight supply now sits where traders usually buy or sell. Since that time long ago, movement has changed shape without loud announcements. Fewer coins available here means something, even if unspoken. Surging traffic across Base, Arbitrum, and Optimism isn’t just speeding things up – each transaction quietly eats into Blob Space. That steady drain is tightening supply, nudging ETH toward scarcity without fanfare. While chains hum louder, the ripple shows up where few expect: deeper down in Ethereum’s economic rhythm. Here’s what experts are watching closely now. A key threshold sits at $2,450 for Ethereum. Should the price stay above that mark past early May, momentum could build fast. The monthly chart shows a formation that traders recognize easily. That shape often leads to moves up, not sideways. Next stop might come in around 3,200. From there, room exists to stretch toward 4,100 before June ends. Opposite really. Selling might seem negative, yet Buterin trades erratic, nearly pointless memecoins into Ethereum instead. That move pulls real demand toward ETH, draining harmful liquidity out. Funding vital research happens along the way, strengthening the network deep down. Out of nowhere, big crypto wallets shift cash – straight from cold storage into staking deals. These moves suggest giants aren’t rushing out anymore. Instead, they’re holding tight, treating ETH like steady income tools built to last. One thing stands out: Bitmine holds 3.45 percent of the total ETH supply, shaping much of the network’s backbone. Because they back the long-term outlook so firmly, prices tend to stabilize even when markets shake hard. Smaller players benefit quietly, shielded from sudden crashes by that steady presence. Though control concentrated in one place raises eyebrows now and then, confidence grows because actions match words over time. Out past the current limits, Verkle Trees step in. These structures unlock what folks call stateless validation – meaning one day, Ethereum might handle countless transactions each second through its layered networks. Even then, anyone could still operate a personal node at home, no supercomputer needed. Most big moves on Lookonchain show clear actions – like Whale A sending $50 million to Binance – but leave the reasons open. Instead of jumping to conclusions, each person must decide what it means. By 2026, patterns point toward institutions adjusting holdings or locking tokens away. These shifts aren’t just panic selling; context matters more now. Title: 'That entire story is false!' Irate Pete Hegseth shouts down Elizabeth Warren
URL: https://www.rawstory.com/hegseth-2676836269/
Time Published: 2026-04-30T18:55:33Z
Full Content:
Tom Boggioni is a writer, born, raised and living in San Diego — where he attended San Diego State University. Prior to writing for Raw Story, he wrote for FireDogLake, blogged as TBogg, and worked in banking, marketing and construction. Sen. Elizabeth Warren’s ability to grill Secretary of Defense Pete Hegseth during her five minutes of questioning during a Senate Armed Forces Committee hearing was hampered by his raising his voice and loudly talking over her on Thursday afternoon. The Massachusetts Democrat got under Hegseth’s skin when she cited a Financial Times report that the Pentagon chief’s stockbroker attempted to make a “multimillion-dollar investment” in defense stocks before the invasion of Iran. After pressing him about growing concerns about insiders at the Pentagon possibly being involved in massive purchases on the stock market related to the war, which he dismissed out of hand, she brought up his personal finances. “I'm also concerned about recent reporting on your own financial dealings with regard to profiting from the war in Iran,” she began. “The Financial Times reported that your broker tried to buy hundreds of shares in a BlackRock fund invested in defense companies just before the war began. The law clearly prohibits the secretary —.” "That entire story is false!” Hegseth shouted. “[It] has been from the beginning and was made up out of whole cloth. And anybody that looks at it sees how it was worded from the beginning to make it look like I was involved in something I had nothing to do and never have.” As Warren tried to speak, he continued to talk over her, insisting, “So any insinuation that I've ever profited other than serving this nation, what I give, what you give, what others give. I'm not looking for money. I don't do it for money. I don't do it for profit. I don't do it for stocks. And that's part of the reason why I'm able to be effective in this job, because no one owns me.” “No one owns this department, no one owns this president. And we can execute for the American people. And we do,” he added. “So, I'd like to hear you say, did you, through your broker at Morgan Stanley or otherwise, seek to invest in any defense-related funds right before Trump started the Iran war?” she finally asked. “I'll give it to you as a big fat negative,” Hegseth shot back. - YouTube youtu.be ALSO READ: Republicans admit plot to evade historic third Trump impeachment Copyright © 2026 Raw Story Media, Inc. Title: The Outlaws Who Keep the Power Grid Running
URL: https://www.esquire.com/news-politics/a71078454/power-grid-outlaws/
Time Published: 2026-04-30T16:33:14Z
Full Content:
Over the last decade, an army of hard-living adrenaline junkies made a fortune and found a kind of freedom maintaining the nation’s energy infrastructure. It was good while it lasted. Last August, in Seminole, Florida, a suburb of St. Petersburg, a crew of power linemen were replacing a utility pole at a small electrical substation next to a used-car dealership. The temperature was pushing 90 degrees, but the sky was clear; an ideal Florida beach day. Just before 2:00 p.m., they strapped the replacement metal pole to the boom on their truck and hoisted it vertically into the air. Setting a new pole is an act of both muscularity and balletic precision, but for experienced linemen, it’s a typical task. The new pole must be held steady at the bottom while an operator in the truck uses a claw on the end of the boom to maneuver it into a waiting hole. When done right, linemen can replace multiple utility poles in a day, but things in Seminole didn’t go as planned: When the boom operator attempted to grab the pole with the claw, he bumped against it instead, causing the top of the pole to accidentally make contact with the high-voltage transmission lines running overhead. The explosion that followed was so violent it lit the earth itself on fire. Sixty-nine thousand volts, enough to feed the substation, powering entire neighborhoods, diverted from the wire into the new pole and rocketed toward the ground. In the current’s way stood Justin Bryan, a thirty-year-old father and music director at his local church, who was balancing the pole from below. The current ripped through Bryan’s body, killing him. It continued flowing into the ground around the truck, causing the grassy field to burst into flames, smoke billowing skyward. Two other linemen, Jefferey Busch and Matthew Waldrop, fathers in their late twenties and early thirties, were also in contact with the pole and were left crawling on the charred earth, horrifically burned but alive. Bystanders could do little to help; so long as the wires remained energized, electricity could still arc and flow anywhere in the vicinity. I first saw an eyewitness video of the accident’s horrific aftermath this past December, not long after I had arrived at the headquarters of Pack Power Services, a utility-services company nestled among the horse farms of Ohio Amish country. Work had been slow; a thin dusting of snow coated the few dozen bucket trucks and pickups in the lot outside the company’s warehouse. When I started reporting on the lives of linemen late last year, I quickly discovered that although linemen are on the front lines of maintaining the power grid—our connection to medical care, drinking water, cell phones, the Internet, emergency services, lights, heat, you name it—no one knows much about them. Most people assumed I was writing about football. But the people who did know what a lineman was kept telling me to talk to the same person: Pack Power’s owner, Jerry Pack. Colleagues told me that he was the quintessential lineman—an Army vet and a biker; rough around the edges, sure, but loyal to a fault. But he was also more than an archetype. Jerry had helped revolutionize the trade and ridden its ups and downs for years. When I got in touch, Jerry, who looks a bit like the actor Brendan Gleeson if Brendan Gleeson had a graying ZZ Top beard and tattoos down his forearms, invited me to come visit on a Thursday—“That’s when we drink,” he said—and greeted me at the warehouse door with a lit Marlboro Red between his fingers. He had also invited a few other linemen with stories to tell, including Nick Mansfield, from East Texas, who quickly pulled me aside and asked if I wanted to see the “real shit” about being a lineman. He pulled his cell phone from his pocket and hit play on a copy of the Seminole video. I lasted only a few seconds before I had to look away. Nick, who met Jerry years ago on the job and remains one of his closest friends, is a lineman’s lineman. He’s burly and bearded and wears a chain around his neck that, at first glance, appears to be a traditional Christ on the cross but isn’t Jesus at all. Rather, it’s a lineman climbing a utility pole. While the incident in Seminole was barely more than a blip in the local news, he told me linemen across the country had seen the video. There’s an old expression, he said: “Telegraph, telephone, tell a lineman.” Word travels fast in the industry, and the industry, lately, had heard too many stories like this. A lineman dies on the job on average once every two weeks, ranking it among the deadliest professions in America. While Jerry and Nick showed me around the warehouse, we discussed the incident in Seminole. The Pinellas County Sheriff’s Office investigated and determined it was an accident, while OSHA recently levied $49,650 in penalties, which is being contested by the contractor hired by the utility company, Duke Energy. But as the video made its way from the jobsite to the union hall to TikTok, linemen in the comments section became fixated on the fact that the transmission line was energized during the work. Jerry and Nick told me that they would have demanded the utility de-energize the lines before getting started, a precaution that would have stopped all the meters in all the homes and businesses downstream of the substation from spinning—and from generating revenue. (When contacted about the details of the incident, Duke Energy replied with this statement: “The safety of our employees, contractors and customers remains Duke Energy’s highest priority, and we continue to cooperate fully with the ongoing investigation process.”) Jerry understood, all too well, why so many linemen who saw the video scrutinized the safety practices that were in place. The men in Seminole had been doing what’s called “blue sky” work, regular repair and maintenance of the power grid. But the industry’s relentless push toward high speed and low cost were most evident in the niche kind of linework that Jerry specialized in: storm chasing. In this run-and-gun model of linework, independent crews show up whenever and wherever they are needed to get the power back on after hurricanes, blizzards, and wildfires. Multiple linemen described the storm-chasing world to me as the “Wild West,” a place where the allure of big money attracted roguish personalities. For years, the living was good, great even, but to hear Jerry and Nick tell it, the frontier was closing. America’s electrical grid was becoming more critical to daily life than ever, and the opportunity to rake in profits had attracted middleman contractors and private-equity investment. For the storm chasers, that meant pressure to perform ratcheting ever higher, and their livelihoods coming under threat as the incentives to do whatever it took to secure contracts—aboveboard or otherwise—grew stronger. For the rest of us? It meant the future of the grid itself was at stake. As the sun went down, the three of us drove the few minutes to Jerry’s ranch house, where we settled in around the kitchen table. Jerry’s girlfriend, Rita, who had already set out foil trays filled with pigs in a blanket and chicken wings on the stovetop, offered us pint glasses of vodka and cranberry juice. I asked Jerry when things started to change. “About ten years ago,” he said as he filled a paper plate with wings. “Irma was the fire under my ass.” In 2017, Jerry was pulling into his driveway after work one day when he discovered his then-wife, Sally, waiting in the doorway in tears. “It’s cancer,” she told him. Her doctor had just called with the test results they’d been waiting for. Over the previous eighteen years, Jerry had worked his way up from an apprenticeship to doing blue-sky work at a substation as a contractor for a local utility. It was a good job with a regular schedule. Sally’s diagnosis changed everything. He’d need to be available to drive her to treatment and support her when she got home. He decided on the spot to drag up—quit without notice, in lineman-speak—and take to the road. “I’ll just storm-chase,” he told her. At the time, most power-restoration efforts were handled by mutual-aid agreements between utilities. If a hurricane hit Florida, a utility company in North Carolina might send some crews to help, understanding that Florida would return the favor if needed. A few large private energy companies had their own crews. Jerry started putting in calls. If linework is the Wild West, storm chasers are the outlaws of the bunch. They’re the guys lauded as heroes on local-news broadcasts for restoring power after natural disasters, but among their ranks you can find all kinds, from drug-addled ex-felons to former lawyers and everything in between. Some storm chasers, affectionately known as tramps, essentially live on the road full-time, traveling the country in RVs or tricked-out pickup trucks, going wherever they’re needed. They keep two drag bags packed at all times: one for their gear—harnesses, hard hats, and boot spikes for climbing utility poles—and the other stuffed with a week’s worth of clothes. Walker Pettis, a tramp from Washington, told me he’s moved thirty times in the past five years. No sooner had Jerry decided to join their ranks than the strongest hurricane in recent memory began intensifying in the southern Atlantic. The meteorologists called it Irma. “THIS IS AS REAL AS IT GETS,” wrote the National Weather Service in a tweet the night before Irma struck. Residents were urged to flee. In the largest exodus in American history, some seven million Floridians got in their cars and drove north, leaving their homes, businesses, and belongings behind. Jerry, on the other hand, jumped in a truck and headed south toward Miami, amid a caravan of vehicles carrying linemen from across the country. He had gotten a call from an acquaintance asking if he could lead a crew. At his day job before storms, Jerry usually ran with four other guys; in Florida, he’d be in charge of 120. “I was scared to fucking death, dude,” he said. In Miami, Jerry and his crew holed up in a hotel and watched through the windows as Irma transformed the world around them: waves of water breaching the Miami River seawall, palm trees blown horizontal in the gale, alligators wandering suburban sidewalks. Nearly two thirds of the state was left without power. Some sixty thousand linemen had descended on the state, and the utility companies scrambled to get organized. The linemen were happy to wait; they were getting paid whether they left the hotel or not. Chaos is cash, as linemen like to say, and Irma had brought the chaos. President Trump described the response as “the largest assemblage of human beings ever in one area for power.” Linemen faced floodwaters, live wires dangling amid the trees, and bird dogs from the utility companies—the office workers tasked with directing crews to their next work site—pressuring them to work on high-voltage lines in gusts of wind and rain. While they moved from one downed pole or blown-out transformer to the next, Jerry and his crew were approached by elderly couples desperate for help and by countless residents demanding to have their TVs turned back on. In a little over a week, the linemen had fully restored power in a state of twenty million people. Public officials hailed them as heroes. Jerry didn’t feel like a hero until he deposited his paycheck. His stress finally abated. “Don’t sweat it,” he told Sally. “We’re all set.” Irma changed everything for Jerry, and it changed everything for linemen. The traditional mutual-aid model was effective, but it could be slow. Pack Power, which Jerry started after returning from Irma, was one of the country’s first linemen-owned businesses to offer something different: rapid response. Jerry’s guys could be sent anywhere they were needed at a moment’s notice. Within a few years, a post-Irma surge of these kinds of outfits had revolutionized the industry. “Years ago, there were no companies that just did storm work,” said a stockbroker-turned-lineman who left what he described as the “scumbag business” of Wall Street and now runs a storm-recovery business on the East Coast. “Today, I’d only be guessing, but I would say there’s probably close to between six hundred to a thousand companies that do just that.” After we’d been talking for an hour, Randy Wolfe slipped into the kitchen. A slight, quiet guy in his early forties who lived nearby, he sat down on a kitchen stool and pulled a Modelo from some hidden recess of his lime-yellow reflective work jacket. Randy epitomized the stormchasing lineman of the post-Irma era, who were in control of their schedules and making fortunes unthinkable to their blue-sky brethren. His taste in beer may be lowbrow, but he owns a collection of high-end sports cars, including a C5 Corvette and a Lotus Elise. More than once, the quiet roads around Jerry’s house had been lit up by wailing sirens and flashing lights as Randy outran the cops in one of his supercars. Randy described the gaps between jobs like this: “I pick a car and I go to the lake all day. If the phone rings, fine. If it don’t ring, fantastic.” For this new generation of linemen, storm-chasing became as much a lifestyle as a livelihood, built on the twin pillars of money and freedom. “We’re like modern-day pirates,” said Nick. At first, all Jerry had wanted was the freedom to bring Sally to her oncology appointments in Chicago without worrying about sick days or PTO. But once the money started rolling in, he bought a few Porsches and some rental properties and expanded his fleet of bucket trucks. The indoor hot tub he installed at his house was so big he had to build a new room to go around it. In order to maintain this newfound abundance, storm chasers relied on a steady stream of what’s known in the business as Good Storms. What exactly makes a Good Storm is up for debate, though time is the obvious answer. A Good Storm lasts a couple weeks at least. The longest can go on for a month or more. For linemen working sixteen-hour days, seven days a week, a Good Storm can yield a paycheck on par with the average American annual salary. Jerry told me that during Irma—one of the best storms in recent memory—the most experienced guys on his crew, which was unionized, were being paid around $150 an hour, double or even triple the going rate back home. That’s $16,800 a week, not including food, travel, and a place to sleep. Though most linemen will proudly recount stories of residents thanking them for getting their power back on after a disaster, it’s clear they’re not in it for the goodwill alone. “This isn’t a humanitarian effort,” as one lineman told me. Nick’s been doing it for so long he now refers to the Weather Channel as “the Money Channel.” Linemen have been known to quit salaried positions without notice just to join a single storm roster during hurricane season. This wayward streak has long been associated with linework. Nick’s “line dad”—his mentor—told him that the roots of the linemen’s renegade spirit can be found in the Vietnam vets who returned from the war and joined the ranks because they felt out of place in society. But the storm-chasing lifestyle brought it to new heights. After driving long hours in caravans of bucket trucks, linemen ride out storms everywhere from empty hotel rooms to hammocks in the backs of their trucks to military-style tent-and-cot setups known as “man camps.” These overnights offer the chance for a lineman’s worst instincts to flourish—hard drinking, affairs with local women dubbed “bucket bunnies,” even crime. Linemen on storm have been accused of everything from felony assault to trafficking drug shipments in their trucks. “There is a culture in linework that is seedy, romanticized, drug ridden,” the former stockbroker said. “There’s a lot of guys that are looking for the next high.” Nick once had a crewmate who found himself face-to-face with an angry pimp after unceremoniously kicking a sex worker out of his hotel room in the middle of the night. A fight ensued and the pimp ended up both beaten up and under arrest. “The lineman was at work at seven in the fucking morning,” Nick recalled. And yet, despite all the potential for misadventure, the shared unpleasantness of sleepless nights and long waits for food at the mess tent had a way of turning colleagues into comrades. “We meet guys one time,” said Nick, “work a storm for a week, and you become lifelong friends.” Nick’s been sober for half a year now, and all night Jerry subtly topped him off with water, not wanting him to stare at an empty glass while we downed our cocktails. Linework was simply too dangerous to do anything but have each other’s back, Jerry told me. “Your ego can’t get in that.” Randy lit up describing how a crowd of linemen had gathered in awe and respect when he and his father ran into each other on storm and set a utility pole together. “It was one of the coolest things I’ve ever been a part of,” he said, pulling another Modelo from his vest. He’d keep drinking them all night. They were a merry band in those days. They saw themselves as something like outlaws, because they’d found a way to make money hand over fist living an itinerant, rambunctious lifestyle that suited them. They were getting away with something. But the hard-partying, carefree life was about to get a lot harder. “Those days are about gone,” Jerry said, wistfully. Someone with an even better way to turn chaos into cash was riding into town. Jaradda Sparks sensed an opportunity. The forward-thinking lineman from Alabama saw more and more storm chasers following Jerry in opening their own companies. He also saw utilities struggling to manage them. He went straight to the utility companies and promised to save them time and money by aggregating the services of hundreds of smaller storm-chasing companies under the umbrella of his own, Sparks Energy. If a storm hit, a power company only had to make one call, to Sparks, to secure the services of thousands of linemen spread out across the country. No more shopping or logistics; Sparks was a one-stop shop. He charged the utilities a hefty premium, but they were clearly willing to pay: By 2019, he had made enough money to open his own livestock ranch, specializing in breeding “top-caliber bucking bulls” for the rodeo circuit. This new aggregator model initially had a beneficial trickle-down effect. Sparks declined to speak with me for this article, but I heard from many linemen that while his approach was designed to cater to utilities, it also funneled jobs to storm chasers in his network, who could work dozens of storms a year without the legwork of going out and bidding for new business. Jerry grossed $15 million one of the years he worked under Sparks as a subcontractor. But the model was so successful that aggregators soon multiplied, just as the storm-chasing outfits once had, creating competition that made life harder for guys like Jerry. In order to lock in higher premiums from utility companies, aggregators tried to corner the market, securing ironclad contracts that ensured utilities would work only with them. Independent companies found it increasingly challenging to land contracts of their own. In intervening years, the industry took on a new shape. There was still a ton of money to be made, but now there was a lot more competition, a new layer of middlemen, and more complicated contracts. This environment cut into the bottom lines of companies like Pack Power. It also opened the door to bad actors. None of the dozen active linemen I spoke to for this story were willing to go on the record about corruption in the industry, but they’ve worked all over the country and nearly all had stories. One storm chaser told me a company he’d worked for hauled broken-down bucket trucks on wreckers to storms because they were being paid per truck, even if the trucks sat in a parking lot unused. Another lineman told me he had been sent out on a storm crew without the proper tools or dielectric testing to make sure the trucks were insulated in case of an accident. When the crews came back an hour later, unable to work, he still got paid. Less visible backroom deals were also in play. One storm-company owner told me explicitly that he paid $15,000 for an executive at a power company to go on an overseas trip in exchange for better work contracts, only for the executive to ghost him once he had returned. The cutthroat competition in the industry incentivized these tactics, and the problems were perpetuated when word, inevitably, got out. Telegraph, telephone, tell a lineman. These shifting practices may also have put lives at risk. At least one lineman I spoke to who asked not to be named told me that some aggregators were known for hiring “guys who weren’t qualified to do the work and going out with trucks that didn’t have any tooling on them.” Jerry’s business suddenly began to suffer. After a couple of years in the black, he found that he was getting fewer calls from aggregators. He told me that inexperienced, nonunion linemen were willing to work for less money per hour than his guys, brothers in the IBEW. Then it stopped being standard for aggregators to pay linemen for mobilization and travel days, forcing those costs to come out of Jerry’s bottom line instead. All this, and many questioned whether the aggregator model was actually saving anyone any money. Average electricity prices are up more than 30 percent across the country in the last five years alone. Are the changes in linework the reason why? It’s hard to say for certain—but it certainly hasn’t helped. Looking back, the storm chasers should have known the end of their era was near when the bucket bunnies broke the Internet. In 2022, a Florida TikToker revealed that in the weeks following Hurricane Ian, her Tinder account had become overrun with linemen heading to the Sunshine State for storm work. “Everyone saying pray for Florida but this is our tinder right now,” she wrote before the reel revealed that, extensive storm damage aside, things in the state maybe weren’t so bad: There was a stream of handsome, well-paid linemen in town looking for dates. Other users followed suit, posting videos of linemen mobilizing for work outside hotels and in parking lots. Then the “linewives,” the married partners of linemen, arrived, posting their own TikToks railing against the bucket bunnies who were out to steal their men. The melodrama was hugely popular. The bucket-bunny trend lasted for months and was viewed nearly three billion times, landing it just behind the Ice Bucket Challenge as one of the most popular memes of the past few years. It spawned merchandise; online commenters begged for a reality-TV show. The linemen, however, were unenthused. As word got out about how much money they were making, sleeping with women while on storm became a lot less carefree. Nick, in his inimitable way, took the opportunity to explain the other three T’s of linework: “You buy your girl new teeth, new tits, and a brand-new Tahoe, before she walks out on you,” he said. Some apprentices, he told me, stopped letting women know they were linemen. Social media had transformed bucket bunnies, at one time the consummate example of the linemen’s freewheeling lifestyle, into a new kind of peril, one that could lead to divorce or something even worse: child support. Of course, if enough money was flying around that bucket bunnies could sniff it out, it was only a matter of time before the real bloodhounds showed up: Wall Street. In the past few years alone, high-powered private-equity firms like BlackRock and Blackstone have found ways to get their cut. Attracted by the potential for a huge, data-center-driven increase in energy, they’ve spent billions buying up major utility providers in places like Minnesota, Indiana, New Mexico, and abroad. According to one count, some 150 million customers in the U. S. now rely on utilities funded at least in part by private-equity money. Under the PE model, buying into the companies that service those utilities is simply another layer of vertical integration to leverage. Just last year, Apollo Global Management spent roughly $2 billion to acquire a stake in PowerGrid Services, a prominent grid-repair and maintenance company founded by a former lineman in 2016. Investments like these are touted as necessary to improve the grid’s infrastructure. Last July, BlackRock’s CEO, Larry Fink, said as much in an interview on CNBC. “We believe that there’s a need for trillions of dollars investing in infrastructure related to our power grids, AI, the whole digitization of the economy,” said Fink. But how big money’s incursion into the grid will affect both linemen and ratepayers is less clear. According to industry studies, the average length of power outages in 2025 was 40 percent longer than in the three years prior. Part of that is down to the increasing severity and frequency of the storms themselves. But an experienced workforce of linemen is essential to the recovery effort. And for them, private equity has been even worse than for customers. In the last two years alone, nonunion linemen have seen pay shrink, while call times to mobilize for storms have shortened from a few days to a few hours. “In the beginning of my storm-chasing career,” Jerry explained, “you had a reasonable time to get there. See the lady, get packed up, take her out to eat. Get on a plane the next day.” Now, he continued, “I have to have guys at the shop ready to leave at all times.” This sometimes means defying DOT rules limiting the number of hours bucket-truck drivers can spend on the road. Once they’ve arrived, the already fatigued linemen are still expected to work sixteen-hour days, seven days a week, until the job is done. So far, linemen have been spared their Perfect Storm moment; there’s no mass tragedy that defines the trade. Instead, the specter of death arrives in a slow, steady cadence. In April of last year, a lineman was killed in Tennessee when his truck overturned into floodwaters; a few weeks later, four linemen were killed in North Carolina when a city dump truck crashed into their work site; in May, a falling tree in Alabama killed a lineman where he stood; in July, a lineman was electrocuted a few miles from where the Seminole tragedy unfolded a month later; in October, a hit-and-run; November, another electrocution, this time in Louisville. A number of fatal incidents show how pressure to cut corners and use junior crews can make the job even more perilous. In recent years, OSHA has assessed penalties of more than $40,000 and claimed that the lack of proper safety precautions led to at least two separate fatal incidents. In one, an electric company “failed to meet their responsibility to ensure a safe and healthful workplace and ensure the proper supervision of new workers.” That incident, in Alabama, resulted in the death of two nineteen-year-old apprentices. In the other, in Miami, a contractor “failed to use required safeguards before starting work,” and a thirty-two-year-old lineman died. Earlier in the evening, I had asked Jerry about the worst accident he had ever seen. He sighed and slumped slightly in his chair; Nick shot me a glance. Don’t go there. But when I brought it up again later, I got a different response: indignation at how the blame for injuries and deaths always seemed to fall at the feet of the linemen themselves, as opposed to, say, contractors or utility companies pressuring crews to work despite safety concerns. “Doesn’t matter what happens, who dies,” Nick said. “The problem can never get back to the power company.” Despite the contested fine levied by OSHA in the Seminole incident, the families of all three victims have had to fend for themselves, starting GoFundMe campaigns to help cover the cost of their recovery. It’s something Anne-Marie Olivieri, a linewife, has seen all too often. She founded the LineLife Foundation to help raise money for linemen killed on the job and as part of her effort tracks every injury and death she can, compiling lists and sharing them among linemen and their families. According to her, the tallies are rarely complete. “A lot of these are swept under the rug or they don’t get reported,” she told me. “There’s been times where I’ve actually gotten pictures of the accident and the company has threatened me.” Wary of a lawsuit, she declined to discuss the particulars, but her experience informs a fear among linemen that in this new private-equity world, shareholder profit, not outage times or lineman safety, will be the new benchmark for success. Nick, indelibly, described how linemen feel. After an accident, he said, “they’ll post your job before they post your obituary.” Around 11:00 p.m., Jerry pushed back from the kitchen table, stood up, and announced it was time to go to the hot-tub room. In there, Nick lit up a cigar while Jerry showed me his collection of Donald Trump–styled rubber duckies and bottles of Pappy Van Winkle bourbon. Like the Vietnam vets who had found a home in linework before him, Jerry had spun a decent day job into his own American dream: freedom, money, and the open road. But it was fleeting. The salad days of $15 million in gross are long gone. This article appeared in the April/May 2026 issue of Esquiresubscribe So far, Jerry is uninterested in cashing in on the private-equity money-go-round, partly, it seems, due to pride. Money and freedom, both valuable things, aren’t quite the same. “There was a time when I had to roll my own fucking cigarettes,” he told me. “I built this, I made every fucking bit of this.” He’d rather hand off Pack Power Services to his kids than to a Wall Street firm. Chris, his eldest son, is already in a leadership role, handling much of the company’s day-to-day responsibilities, including storm work. PJ, another son, is the in-house mechanic. Jerry’s daughter stands to inherit his rental properties. And while he and Sally, recovered from cancer, split up a few years ago, she lives just a few minutes away in a house Jerry says he paid for. This future is dependent on whether or not the company survives. Like nearly all the other businesses started by former linemen whose owners I spoke to, the injection of big money into the industry has made it difficult for Pack Power to compete. In the good ol’ days, Jerry had millions in the bank and no debt. “Now I’ve got fifty grand in the bank and I’m two million in the hole,” he told me. Jerry still has the lineman’s hustle in his blood. He’s working on a deal to help the FAA modernize the infrastructure at a nearby airport. And there’s this other thing. “As soon as I saw Russia invading Ukraine, I knew we, as the United States, were going to be rebuilding their power grid,” he told me. “I’d love to be on that.” I didn’t know how seriously to take him, considering we were surrounded by empty beer cans and a haze of cigar and cigarette smoke. But as it turns out, Jerry had in fact spent much of the past year developing plans with a partner in the UK to join the international effort to rebuild Ukraine’s power grid, whenever that day comes. It was nearing midnight when I left Jerry’s, the image of a crew of American linemen stepping off a plane in postwar Kyiv, just as they’d rushed into California after a wildfire or Florida after Irma, running through my head. The Wild West had been tamed, so they’d have to go find a new frontier. Outside, it was dark; there weren’t many streetlights in the neighborhood. But as I drove past the Pack Power warehouse on the way to my hotel, it was a different sight that stirred something in me: a long row of bucket trucks parked in the lot out front, ready and waiting for the next job, still coated in snow but illuminated under the warehouse lights. Title: Here's Who Really Owns All Of The Major Tech Brands
URL: https://www.bgr.com/2156761/who-really-owns-major-tech-brands/
Time Published: 2026-04-30T14:02:00Z
Full Content:
Technology has become an integral part of how we live. For work and play, we rely on our smartphones, computers, and other tech-powered devices. Today, many of the world's biggest companies are built around technology. They shape everything for us, from communication to entertainment to how businesses operate. Major tech brands may appear to be fierce competitors — Apple versus Samsung, Google versus Meta, and so on — but behind the scenes, the reality is far more interconnected. Many of the biggest tech brands are not independent. Most of them, in fact, are controlled by the same handful of powerful companies and stakeholders. The truth behind who owns the biggest tech brands in the world isn't just trivia. It reveals a lot about the brands and what they sell. From social media giants to gaming and cloud platforms, we'll take a closer look at who really owns the brands whose products we use every day. Since its founding in 1993, NVIDIA has become one of the biggest tech companies, with a market capitalization of $4.48 trillion as of January 2026. It's the first company to hit that exorbitant milestone, and today, it's regarded as the backbone of the AI revolution. It essentially controls the discrete graphics processing unit (GPU) market, and it runs the world's most advanced AI systems and data centers. NVIDIA chips power everything from ChatGPT to self-driving cars, making it one of the world's most valuable tech companies. NVIDIA isn't owned by a single person or company. It's a publicly traded firm, with majority ownership split among institutional investors like The Vanguard Group and BlackRock, as well as individual stakeholders. Vanguard is owned by its member funds, which are held by their investors. This means shareholders are the actual owners. Nearly 75% of BlackRock is owned by large investment firms like Vanguard, State Street, and Capital Group. CEO Larry Link owns a small personal stake of 0.5%. The remaining 24.5% is with public shareholders. The co-founder and CEO of NVIDIA, Jen-Hsun (Jensen) Huang, is its largest individual shareholder. Huang owns more than 922 million shares, which means about 4% of NVIDIA. This gives him a net worth of roughly $145 billion as of March 2026. Founded in 1998, Google is one of the most powerful tech brands in the world. Its core product holds around 90% of the global search engine market as of 2026. Google also offers services like Maps, Gmail, and Android, and the brand develops consumer electronics like Pixel smartphones, Fitbit wearables, and Nest smart home devices. More recently, Google has branched out into artificial intelligence with its Gemini AI models. In regard to its corporate structure, Google is owned by Alphabet, Inc., a holding company that co-founders Larry Page and Sergey Brin created in 2015 to restructure Google. Alphabet's ownership follows a three-class share structure: Class A, Class B, and Class C. Class A shares are publicly traded and mainly owned by institutional investors. This mainly includes Vanguard Group, BlackRock, and FMR, which jointly own around 20% of Class A shares and around 16% of Class C shares. Class B shares are not available for public trading. They are private, non-traded stock primarily controlled by co-founders Larry Page (45%) and Sergey Brin (42%). Investor and venture capitalist L. John Doerr also owns about 2.6% of the outstanding shares. YouTube operates as a wholly owned subsidiary of Google's parent company, Alphabet, Inc. In November 2006, Google bought YouTube for $1.65 billion. The deal raised eyebrows at the time, but today, it's known as one of the most successful acquisitions ever. YouTube has since evolved into the world's dominant video platform and a key advertising engine. It reaches about 2.7 billion users each month, which makes it one of the most influential media platforms of all time. It also happens to be one of Alphabet's biggest revenue drivers. In 2025, YouTube generated $60 billion, far surpassing its biggest streaming rival, Netflix (which made $45 billion). YouTube generates money mainly from advertising and YouTube Premium subscriptions, and it's a strong pillar of the creator economy. While it started as a simple video-sharing site, YouTube has established itself as a global media giant that competes with TV networks, streaming services, and social media — to ultimately grow the net worth of Alphabet's stakeholders. Instagram is owned by Meta Platforms (formerly known as Facebook), a publicly traded company listed on the Nasdaq exchange. Meta acquired Instagram in 2012 for approximately $1 billion — a deal that's regarded as one of the best tech acquisitions in history. Under Meta, Instagram has grown from a basic photo-sharing app into a global creator and advertising powerhouse. With over 2 billion monthly active users, it's the third most popular social network after Facebook and WhatsApp. Instagram is all about short-form video (Reels), Stories, and the influencer-driven content popular among younger generations. This plays a central role in Meta's growth strategy. In fact, Instagram is one of Meta's biggest advertising engines. With its interactive visual format and deep integration with shopping features, Instagram has become a key platform for brands, influencers, and businesses looking to reach global audiences. Mark Zuckerberg, the founder and original developer of Facebook, owns about 15.7% of Meta. Large institutional investors like Vanguard, BlackRock, and State Street hold more than 75% ownership. Here's a big tech brand that needs no introduction. We've all been using Facebook for years, and we know that it's the flagship product of Meta Platforms, Inc. In 2004, Mark Zuckerberg launched Facebook so Harvard students could share profiles, photos, and class schedules. And at present, with over 3 billion active users, Facebook is the largest social network globally. Even though it may no longer be the trendiest app, many still rely on it to connect with friends and family — and Facebook still drives the majority of Meta's advertising revenue despite growing competition from other social platforms. The founder, Mark Zuckerberg, owns about 15.7% of Meta. Meanwhile, asset managers like Vanguard, BlackRock, and State Street hold over 75% of the company. Beyond social networking, Facebook has expanded to feature groups, marketplaces, events, and video content. This means that it's more of a multipurpose platform than a traditional social media app. In 2024, Facebook earned $164.5 billion in revenue, with approximately $91 billion coming from the Facebook app. A large chunk of that revenue comes from advertising, giving Facebook a market share that rivals Google's and Amazon's. In 1994, Jeff Bezos founded Amazon as an online bookstore. Today, Amazon dominates global e-commerce and cloud computing. Over time, the company has expanded into cloud computing and entertainment, and Amazon Web Services (AWS) is now the most comprehensive and broadly adopted cloud platform across the globe. With multiple successful business segments, Amazon is among the most recognizable tech brands in the world. In 2025, it generated $717 billion in revenue, making it the largest company in the world by revenue. And around 18% of that revenue was driven by AWS. Prime Video, meanwhile, is a strong contender in the streaming space; there are over 250 million Prime members in 27 countries. Amazon also owns Zoox, an autonomous technology company that makes self-driving vehicles. It seems the company has no plans to slow its growth. Bezos took Amazon public in 1997, but he still remains the largest individual shareholder with approximately 8.8% of shares valued at more than $200 billion. Like most big tech companies, Amazon is heavily owned by major institutional investors as well. BlackRock, Vanguard Group, and State Street own an estimated 18% to 20% of Amazon. These firms aren't the sole controlling owners. Rather, they invest on behalf of millions of clients worldwide. Known as a top-tier manufacturer of electric cars, Tesla makes a number of vehicles that feature Full Self-Driving (FSD) and autopilot assistance technologies. These vehicles can steer, accelerate, and brake automatically, requiring fewer user inputs on the road. Bestselling high-performance electric vehicles like Tesla's Model 3 and Model Y make it the most satisfying EV brand to drive. Tesla also manufactures industrial batteries and solar products to store clean energy. Tesla is a publicly traded company listed on the Nasdaq Global Select Market. Co-founder and CEO Elon Musk is Tesla's largest shareholder and also its most influential figure. Musk's decisions and public persona have a direct impact on Tesla's stock and strategy. And with more than 500 million shares, he owns around 15% of the company. This makes Musk the richest man on Earth with a net worth of $785.5 billion at the time of this writing. Vanguard, BlackRock, and State Street are all among the largest asset managers with stakes in Tesla, Inc. TikTok, founded by ByteDance, is a popular social media platform where users can create, share, and discover short-form videos. It was initially released in September 2016 in China as Douyin, but in 2017, it was launched in international markets as TikTok. The platform gained popularity in 2018 and 2019 after it collaborated with Musical.ly, and then it achieved global dominance during the COVID-19 pandemic in 2020. To say that TikTok disrupted YouTube and Instagram with its algorithm would be an understatement. Today, TikTok is one of the most popular and fastest-growing social platforms, with around 1.99 billion monthly active users worldwide. ByteDance is still a privately held company based in China, but it doesn't currently own TikTok in the U.S. As of 2026, the American version of TikTok is owned by the newly formed TikTok USDS Joint Venture LLC, which is mostly backed by U.S. investors. More than 80% of the company is owned by American and global entities. This includes Oracle Corporation, a trusted security partner for storing all U.S. user data in the cloud; Silver Lake, a technology-focused private equity firm; and MGX, an Abu Dhabi state-owned investment group. The Chinese parent company ByteDance still has a 19.9% stake in it. In 2011, Twitch started as a spin-off of a 24/7 reality television show by Justin.tv, Inc. In August 2014, Amazon acquired it for nearly $970 million. At that point, Twitch was a niche platform focusing only on video games. But under Amazon's leadership, it has grown to become a leading live-streaming platform that features gaming, music, electronic sports, talk shows, and even real-time lifestyle streaming. As of early 2025, Twitch had over 240 million active users. The tech brand earned $1.8 billion in revenue in 2024 and hosted more than 7.1 million active streaming channels in 2025. Twitch is also an important part of its parent company's broader media strategy. It offers several benefits as hidden perks for Amazon Prime members, who can claim a free monthly subscription to a channel on Twitch. Members are also entitled to free in-game content like virtual currency and characters for popular games like FIFA. These cross-platform benefits help Twitch retain users and creators within its ecosystem despite growing competition from YouTube and Kick. Amazon is a publicly traded company listed on the Nasdaq. It is also owned by a mix of individual insiders and institutional investors. Most people might not be familiar with Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company (TSMC), but it is one of the biggest semiconductor companies in 2026. TSMC is the world's largest and most important pure-play foundry, and it single-handedly makes Taiwan the country that produces the most computer chips for companies like Apple, NVIDIA, and AMD. TSMC is a key player in the global AI and technology supply chain, as it holds around 60% to 70% of the global market share. In 2025, TSMC grew almost four times faster than its rivals. And with a market cap of about $2 trillion, TSMC is the second-largest semiconductor company and the sixth most valuable company by market cap. Like many global corporations, TSMC is publicly traded, and its ownership is spread across investors. The Taiwanese government is the largest individual shareholder; it owns roughly 6.38% of the company. Foreign institutional investors hold around 70% to 75% of the shares, primarily consisting of global asset managers, sovereign wealth funds, banks, mutual funds, and index funds from the U.S. and Europe. Other shareholders include domestic Taiwanese institutional investors and retail investors. Founded in 1976, Apple is the second-largest company by market valuation today. It stands right after NVIDIA, and beats other tech giants like Google and Microsoft. The Cupertino, California-based brand originally started with the Apple I, a hand-assembled computer — but it's now known for products like the iPhone, iPad, Mac, and Apple Watch. Apple's services businesses, meanwhile, include iCloud, Apple Arcade, Apple Music, and Apple TV+. In 2018, Apple became the world's first company to reach a market value of $3 trillion. As of 2026, Apple has a net worth of around $4 trillion. The iPhone is Apple's most popular product and its primary revenue driver. In 2025, Apple made $416 billion in revenue, and half of it came directly from iPhones. Initially, Steve Jobs held about 11% of Apple, but he later sold most of his shares after leaving the company. Today, CEO Tim Cook owns less than 1% of Apple. Most shares are held by major institutional investors like Vanguard Group, State Street Corporation, Berkshire Hathaway, and BlackRock. Berkshire Hathaway is a publicly traded company formerly led by the American philanthropist Warren Buffett. He holds a significant voting control through the share structure. IBM came into existence in 1911, which makes it one of the oldest names in the tech industry. The company has continuously reinvented itself to stay relevant all these years. Initially, it started with business-based systems like punch-card tabulating machines. During the 1960s and '70s, IBM was the unrivaled leader in computing, producing roughly 70% of the world's computers. IBM is best known for its powerful mainframes, which you can still find in banks, governments, and large enterprises. But IBM has shifted its focus to enterprise software and consulting business. In recent years, it has also entered other markets like hybrid cloud computing, robotics, research, and artificial intelligence. When it comes to revenue, IBM doesn't compete with the rest of Big Tech. The company brought in around $67 billion in 2025. Current chairman and CEO, Arvind Krishna, owns 371,894 shares — but this gives him only a tiny 0.04% stake in IBM. The majority of IBM shares (around 64%) are owned by big asset managers like Vanguard, BlackRock, and State Street. WhatsApp is owned by Meta Platforms as a privately held subsidiary and does not trade independently on the Nasdaq as its parent company does. Meta acquired the messaging platform in 2014 for about $19 billion in cash and stock. This surpassed Google's $3.2 billion purchase of Nest Labs, and also Apple's $3 billion deal with Beats Electronics, to become one of the biggest tech buys ever. Today, WhatsApp is one of Meta's most valuable assets. Initially, WhatsApp was designed as a simple, ad-free messaging service. Unlike Facebook and Instagram, WhatsApp is focused on private, end-to-end encrypted communication. WhatsApp is a cross-platform messaging service that allows you to send messages, make calls, and share media between Android, iOS, Windows, and Mac devices. As of now, WhatsApp has nearly 3 billion active monthly users across the globe. On average, 100 billion messages and 7 billion voice notes are shared on WhatsApp every day. It has become the default messaging app in many parts of the world, and Meta has gradually begun to monetize it via business tools, customer communication features, and payment integrations. Founded in 1968, Intel was an early pioneer of the semiconductor industry. We now know Intel for making central processing units (CPUs) for personal computers, enterprise systems, and servers. It also makes motherboard chipsets, flash memory, solid state drives (SSDs), and network interface controllers. For decades, Intel dominated the semiconductor industry, especially when it came to PCs. In recent years, though, it has faced intense competition from TSMC, AMD, and NVIDIA. To remain competitive in today's market, Intel has followed a transformational strategy called IDM 2.0, which shifts it from a chipmaker to a systems foundry that can also produce chips for other companies like NVIDIA and AMD. Today, Intel is also actively making neural processing units (NPUs) for AI tasks. Intel is a publicly traded company that's predominantly owned by large investment companies (47%) and individual shareholders (around 50%). The Vanguard Group holds the largest stake with 8.8%, and BlackRock holds roughly 6%. In August 2025, the U.S. government completed a deal to acquire a roughly 10% stake in Intel. Microsoft owns Xbox, a premium video game brand that features hardware consoles (Series X, S, One), Game Pass subscriptions, and cloud gaming. Consumers can play, buy, and stream games across consoles, PCs, and even their mobile phones. While Xbox is not separately listed on the Nasdaq, its parent company, Microsoft, is a publicly traded company. Xbox was Microsoft's first step into the world of console gaming, and the brand has now expanded beyond traditional consoles. In October 2023, Microsoft finalized a $69 billion deal with Activision Blizzard, and this has played a big role in Xbox's success. Blockbuster titles like "Call of Duty" and "Candy Crush" brought a surge of 49% in Xbox revenue. In 2025, Microsoft generated a record revenue of $281.7 billion. Co-founder Bill Gates is still a shareholder in Microsoft, but his stake has dropped to around 1.5%. Currently, institutional investors own approximately 72% of outstanding shares: Vanguard has 9.1%, BlackRock has 7.5%, and State Street has 4.2%. CEO Satya Nadella and Vice President Bradford L. Smith together hold less than 1% of the company. WeChat is owned and developed by Tencent Holdings Company, a Chinese technology conglomerate. It was initially started as a simple messaging app for China — basically the Chinese equivalent of WhatsApp. But through strategic development, Tencent has transformed WeChat from a simple messenger app into a super app for China. WeChat now works as an all-in-one platform for messaging, social networking, payments, and services. Essentially, it offers everything users need in their everyday life: They can shop, book flights, rent bikes, and play games via mini-programs that work like built-in apps and don't require additional downloads. WeChat has over 1.4 billion monthly users and, between January and July 2025, the company made $81.19 million from in-app purchases. The Dutch investment group Prosus N.V. is Tencent's largest shareholder with approximately 23% of the company. Prosus is owned by Naspers, which originally invested $32 million in Tencent in 2001. Tencent's co-founder and CEO, Ma Huateng, holds roughly 8% to 9% of shares. And 56% is owned by various institutional and retail investors like Vanguard and HSBC Custody. Samsung is a global leader in consumer electronics and semiconductor manufacturing. It is best known for making smartphones, TVs, and display technology, which its customers use worldwide. According to reported customer satisfaction, Samsung is one of the best smart TV brands. The South Korean giant also supplies key components to rival tech brands like Apple and Xiaomi. In Q4 of 2025, Samsung hit a record-high revenue of KRW 93.8 trillion (which is equal to approximately $63.38 billion). When it comes to its ownership, Samsung is very different from most Western tech companies. Samsung is part of a South Korean chaebol, a type of family-controlled conglomerate. Samsung is owned by the Lee family, originally led by Lee Byung-chul and later Lee Kun-hee. Although Samsung Electronics is publicly traded, the founding Lee family still maintains control through complex cross-shareholdings. Basically, Samsung works like both a tech giant and a family-run empire. Institutional investors such as the National Pension Service of Korea (one of the largest public pension funds) and BlackRock also have key stakes in Samsung.

Title: How Does a Bond Ladder Work?
URL: https://awealthofcommonsense.com/2026/04/how-does-a-bond-ladder-work/
Time Published: 2026-04-30T13:54:27Z
Full Content:
A Wealth of Common Sense Posted April 30, 2026 by Ben Carlson A reader asks: What do you think of Build Better Bond Ladders with iShares – BlackRock, for building bond ladders instead of using a bond fund/ETF such as BND? How exactly do they work when they mature and send the money back to you in terms of the money you get back? If I buy $1K of the ETF, do I get the $1K back in the year the ETF matures? Since you can buy/sell at any time, it doesn’t seem that the value would stay stable like it would if I bought a bond directly. There are a lot of target maturity bond funds now. Invesco has them. State Street has these bonds. Vanguard too. I’m sure there are others. iShares has a tool that allows you to build a bond ladder using these ETFs: You can toggle between different types of bonds — corporates, treasuries, TIPS, munies and high yield — while defining how far out you’d like to go out in terms of maturity. As you change between different types of bonds and maturity levels you can see how it will impact your yield, duration, etc. It’s pretty neat that you can do this as opposed to going in and buying the individual bonds yourself (which can be tricky if you’ve never done it before). So why would you buy bond funds with a target maturity? The reason for these bonds is twofold: 1. You have a specific goal that you need money for at a specific time. If you know you need the money in 3 years for a house down payment, you could invest in a 2029 fund. If you know you’ll be paying your child’s college tuition bill in 5 years, you could buy a fund with a 2031 maturity date. 2. You want to create a bond ladder. A bond ladder is a fixed income strategy where you stagger the maturity dates of your portfolio rather than putting your money into a perpetual bond fund or a single security. Let’s say you have $100,000 to invest. Instead of investing it all in a single bond or fund you might create a bond ladder that goes out 5 years. You put $20,000 to work using funds with maturity dates of 2027, 2028, 2029, 2030 and 2031. It’s called a bond ladder because each maturity level is a step up to a new rung. When those bond funds mature you could either use the proceeds for spending purposes or reinvest them back into a new bond with a 5 year maturity. What’s the point of a bond ladder? When you buy a fund like BND it’s perpetual, meaning the fund never fully matures. Currently, BND has an average maturity of around 8 years and an average duration of 5.7 years. Those numbers can change a bit as the types of bonds in the fund may evolve over time. But the maturity of a perpetual bond fund like this never approaches zero, unlike an individual bond or one of the target-maturity ETFs. The bond managers sell securities in the fund and buy new ones as they mature, keeping the average duration/maturity relatively consistent. If rates rise, a fund like BND can fall in value, and it could take some time to recover. Even with income included, BND sustained a decent drawdown when rates rose in 2022: In a recessionary environment, you would expect to see the opposite, with rates falling and this fund giving your portfolio a boost. You won’t get much of a boost in a bond ladder unless you sell the funds, which defeats the purpose. The underlying target maturity funds will still see some movement in price as variables such as interest rates, inflation and economic growth change. But as they approach maturity, the volatility falls. Price sensitivity is lower as the par payout becomes more predictable, regardless of market conditions. And yes, you should more or less receive par value at maturity assuming there are no defaults along the way. A bond ladder can help when it comes to interest rate risk since you would be diversifying your entry points at different rate levels. In many ways, a bond ladder is a form of dollar-cost averaging across different yields and maturities. However, there is reinvestment risk if you reinvest the proceeds of a maturing fund and current yields are lower. A bond ladder can make sense for any investor who desires stable, predictable cash flows without trying to concentrate in a single point on the yield curve. There is more maintenance involved with a bond ladder as well. You have to reinvest the maturing bonds, determine how far out you’ll go on the maturity spectrum, choose the types of bonds you want to invest in and so forth. It’s a little more complicated. If you need money at a specific time or you just like the comfort of knowing when your bonds mature, a bond ladder can make sense. Many retirees like a bond ladder for peace of mind. If you would prefer more general fixed income exposure with less active management, something like BND or another bond fund makes more sense. One option is not better or worse than the other. They each have pros and cons.
